    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<span id="freeTextContainer17635940000412372674">Sampath Chawla was born in a time of drought that ended with a vengeance the night of his birth. All signs being auspicious, the villagers triumphantly assured Sampath's proud parents that their son was destined for greatness. <br/><br/>Twenty years of failure later, that unfortunately does not appear to be the case. A sullen government worker, Sampath is inspired only when in searc</span>
  <span id="freeText17635940000412372674" style="display:none">Sampath Chawla was born in a time of drought that ended with a vengeance the night of his birth. All signs being auspicious, the villagers triumphantly assured Sampath's proud parents that their son was destined for greatness. <br /><br />Twenty years of failure later, that unfortunately does not appear to be the case. A sullen government worker, Sampath is inspired only when in search of a quiet place to take his nap. "But the world is round," his grandmother says. "Wait and see Even if it appears he is going downhill, he will come up the other side. Yes, on top of the world. He is just taking a longer route." No one believes her until, one day, Sampath climbs into a guava tree and becomes unintentionally famous as a holy man, setting off a series of events that spin increasingly out of control. A delightfully sweet comic novel that ends in a raucous bang, <i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i> is as surprising and entertaining as it is beautifully wrought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17635940000412372674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/104975575?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=religion-and-sprituality">religion-and-sprituality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer104975575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5769018841765851524">Chauncey Gardener in India, Sampath is a slacker of the first order, completely lacking in ambition and as distractible as an infant confronted with moving shiny objects. After losing his job in a rather dramatic manner, Sampath wanders up the road until he feels the pull of a guava tree and decides to take up residence. The unknowing manage to project onto him a fully undeserved holiness. A new cult is born as Sampath tosses out meaningless parables in answer to the many questions asked him by </span>
  <span id="freeText5769018841765851524" style="display:none">Chauncey Gardener in India, Sampath is a slacker of the first order, completely lacking in ambition and as distractible as an infant confronted with moving shiny objects. After losing his job in a rather dramatic manner, Sampath wanders up the road until he feels the pull of a guava tree and decides to take up residence. The unknowing manage to project onto him a fully undeserved holiness. A new cult is born as Sampath tosses out meaningless parables in answer to the many questions asked him by the gullible. Desai clearly has a bone to pick with the practice of religion (and the postal service) in India. This is a fun read, but although I thought I was catching much of what was tossed out to the reader there must be a lot that my western eyes missed that is perceived by South Asian natives. It is an entertaining and fast read. A criminal monkey and his pack are particularly entertaining. In an American context they might stand in for Republicans aligning with religious frenzy for their own ends. I expect the same breed exists in India. Not a must read, but an entertaining one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5769018841765851524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer177964185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13148028389774844039">I try my best to respond to the text and not to other readers here, but really negative reviewers? REALLY!? The book is called <i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i>. Not since <i>Breakin' II: Electric Boogaloo</i> has a work so clearly announced itself as ridiculous. Did you read the back cover? The one that says plainly that this is the tale of a life-long loser who climbs a tree to escape the bastards only to be mistaken for a holy man which is all well and good until the drunken monkeys show up? No, reall</span>
  <span id="freeText13148028389774844039" style="display:none">I try my best to respond to the text and not to other readers here, but really negative reviewers? REALLY!? The book is called <i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i>. Not since <i>Breakin' II: Electric Boogaloo</i> has a work so clearly announced itself as ridiculous. Did you read the back cover? The one that says plainly that this is the tale of a life-long loser who climbs a tree to escape the bastards only to be mistaken for a holy man which is all well and good until the drunken monkeys show up? No, really--the monkeys are paste-eating drunk. Truly. And you picked this book up thinking just <i>perhps</i> it was your cup o' tea, Satre lover? C'mon negative reviewer: you're not fooling anyone here. No one but a blue-blooded ass-clown would pick this book up without knowing exactly what it is. And, for what it is, it's pretty fantastic. I mean sure, there's nearly enough here--the outsider story, the satire of small-town life and politics, the failed hero's journey--to make this a teachable book, but it is from start to finish the delightfully ridiculous book it over-and-over announces itself to be. So if you read this and didn't like it, that's <i>your</i> bad. Don't go sand-bagging a delightful book because you don't like fables or magical realism or drunken monkeys. Don't walk into a shot bar with a neon sign that reads "Tooters for Hooters" expecting a craft brew, ya ridiculous drunken monkey, you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13148028389774844039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_599817390" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/599817390?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571195718" href="/book/show/916891.Hullabaloo_in_the_Guava_Orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=indian-novels">indian-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer599817390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16104676740887679036">Fairly amusing and fairly brief novel about Sampath, an Indian adolescent, who really does not want to work hard and who would rather laze around. One day he suddenly decides he would like to sit at the top of a guava tree. He stays there and refuses to come down. He begins to be mistaken for a wise man. <br/>There is an air of predictability about this and some of the characters are very formulaic. <br/>There are some very funny moments though and the saga of the drunken monkeys is hilarious. Desai also </span>
  <span id="freeText16104676740887679036" style="display:none">Fairly amusing and fairly brief novel about Sampath, an Indian adolescent, who really does not want to work hard and who would rather laze around. One day he suddenly decides he would like to sit at the top of a guava tree. He stays there and refuses to come down. He begins to be mistaken for a wise man. <br />There is an air of predictability about this and some of the characters are very formulaic. <br />There are some very funny moments though and the saga of the drunken monkeys is hilarious. Desai also very neatly dissects bureaucracy and the inability of local dignitaries to make decisions. She also sends up the role of the guru mercilessly and some of the sayings Sampath passes on to those who visit him in his tree are close enough to the sort of things you read in books of wisdom/proverbs to be convincing and amusing at the same time. Desai must have had great fun making them up: Remember "If you do not weed your tomato plant will not flower".<br />The plot is a little thin at times and some of the interesting side stories would have benefited from expansion. The ending doesn't work, but on the whole it is enjoyable and doesn't stretch the mind too much; which is sometimes a good thing, especially as I'm going to read Middle C next!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16104676740887679036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Praxedes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Praxedes" href="/user/show/4413597-praxedes">Praxedes</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1637676160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17165432817938940854">What a great balance between wit and lovely literature! I was often reminded of the Magic Realism genre while perusing this work, with it's almost plausible absurdities, a vast array of main characters (mostly related to each other), and its odd reactions to common day events.<br/><br/>I have noticed that unlike their Western counterparts --where there is one or at most two main characters-- most Indian novels feature families or neighborhoods as protagonists. For those of us largely unfamiliar with India</span>
  <span id="freeText17165432817938940854" style="display:none">What a great balance between wit and lovely literature! I was often reminded of the Magic Realism genre while perusing this work, with it's almost plausible absurdities, a vast array of main characters (mostly related to each other), and its odd reactions to common day events.<br /><br />I have noticed that unlike their Western counterparts --where there is one or at most two main characters-- most Indian novels feature families or neighborhoods as protagonists. For those of us largely unfamiliar with Indian names and surnames it takes a bit of practice to determine who is who, but in this case it is totally worth it. Desai's skill as a writer is undeniable, and how she turns hyperbole into humor is commendable.<br /><br />This is a quick read that packs a wallop!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17165432817938940854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2329761038" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2329761038" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2329761038?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2329761038" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11877823830424813420">The novel begins with the most classical of Indian scenes; that of the murky, muggy monsoon rains; the novel deals with the most eponymous of Indian characters-the spoiled, feckless only son. Somewhere in between this Kiran Desai is able to write a humorous, if not exactly ground-breaking, story of Sampath, a diffident ditherer whose foray into a guava tree transforms him into a a sham spiritualist for the gullible and naive.<br/><br/>Like many only sons Sampath is born under the weighty lode of familial </span>
  <span id="freeText11877823830424813420" style="display:none">The novel begins with the most classical of Indian scenes; that of the murky, muggy monsoon rains; the novel deals with the most eponymous of Indian characters-the spoiled, feckless only son. Somewhere in between this Kiran Desai is able to write a humorous, if not exactly ground-breaking, story of Sampath, a diffident ditherer whose foray into a guava tree transforms him into a a sham spiritualist for the gullible and naive.<br /><br />Like many only sons Sampath is born under the weighty lode of familial expectations stemming from a culture of mindless, unquestioning patriarchy; his shoulders, unable to cope with the weight gradually slope into a life-time of failure-as a student, as a worker where an impromptu act of disrobing serves at the wedding of his employer's daughter acts as the sole act of merit in a lifetime of mediocrity. For reasons inexplicable even to himself, Sampath somehow finds some meaning in a guava fruit given to him by his mother-this soon leads to him climbing a guava tree where he posits himself in all his glory. Eventually he begins to mouth bland platitudes and gains the reputation as a guru-the line between a mad-man and mystic can be  as thin as the branch of a guava tree and so his reputation gradually grows until he become famous as a wise sage. Nothing fundamentally changes about Sampath's character, but what changes is how is perceived; his notoriety gives weight to his blandishments, until his reputation is brought low by a group of ribaldrous monkeys, whose simian escapades serve to undermine the reputation he has so carefully cultivated. <br /><br />The novel at times blazes forth with brief bursts of beauty and is host to a side cast of weird and wonderful caricatures; the long-suffering mother, the vivacious sister and her dopey lover, the over-bearing father and there is a charm to the story which perhaps makes the reader forget it's lack of substance, outside of exploring the often ridiculous nature of the guru's who crop up around India, with their empty, pointless pontifications and sham spiritualism. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11877823830424813420" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Em*bedded-in-books*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2481560-em-bedded-in-books"><img alt="Em*bedded-in-books*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605433673p2/2481560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/244461092?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Em*bedded-in-books*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Em*bedded-in-books*" href="/user/show/2481560-em-bedded-in-books">Em*bedded-in-books*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who love Indian fiction</span></div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8627218759254169208">This had an absurd storyline with eccentric characters. I never thought I would like it as much as I did because I am not inclined towards satire and silliness. This book made me realize that beautiful writing can surpass other faults. This is the story of Sampath, an eccentric young man born to a mad woman who has a long genealogy of craziness. He is overwhelmed by ordinary routine and the need for a steady paying job and just runs off to the forest one day and starts living in the branches of </span>
  <span id="freeText8627218759254169208" style="display:none">This had an absurd storyline with eccentric characters. I never thought I would like it as much as I did because I am not inclined towards satire and silliness. This book made me realize that beautiful writing can surpass other faults. This is the story of Sampath, an eccentric young man born to a mad woman who has a long genealogy of craziness. He is overwhelmed by ordinary routine and the need for a steady paying job and just runs off to the forest one day and starts living in the branches of a guava tree. Little does he realize that his whole family will follow, camp out in the forest and try to take advantage of the situation. As a bonus, a group of rowdy monkeys befriend Sampath. The hullabaloo is complete when they are all joined forces by the local reporters, police, army and bureocracy.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/539102725?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sairam Krishnan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sairam Krishnan" href="/user/show/8056568-sairam-krishnan">Sairam Krishnan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571195718" href="/book/show/916891.Hullabaloo_in_the_Guava_Orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer539102725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6559610597535744852">At least two of my friends say that they don't like Kiran Desai's writing. I don't understand them. I read 'The Inheritance of Loss' before I read this, and I absolutely loved it. It spoke to an angst in me that I myself couldn't have given words to, and there are several passages in the book I hold very close to me.<br/><br/>'Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard' is a very different book; it's an incredible story and written with an amazing mastery of words.<br/>It's not an easily believable story, there're elemen</span>
  <span id="freeText6559610597535744852" style="display:none">At least two of my friends say that they don't like Kiran Desai's writing. I don't understand them. I read 'The Inheritance of Loss' before I read this, and I absolutely loved it. It spoke to an angst in me that I myself couldn't have given words to, and there are several passages in the book I hold very close to me.<br /><br />'Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard' is a very different book; it's an incredible story and written with an amazing mastery of words.<br />It's not an easily believable story, there're elements of the fantastic and as she scythes through the idiosyncrasies of small town India with a razor sharp eye, you actually flinch. Shahkot's pompous middle aged men, rotund aunties, lazy government officers and 'eligible' bachelors come and go in passages that rake through arranged marriage, Bollywood-love and small town stupidity. <br /><br />There is not one loose end, every character comes full circle, you are never left wondering what happen to him or her, and the chaos at the end of the novel, laugh-out-loud funny though it is, again reflects the writer's skill in the control of the narrative.<br /><br />She will remain one of my favorite writers. And if you are sitting around wondering if you should read this book, let me tell you something - you certainly should.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6559610597535744852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1798851205" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1798851205" />
    <a title="Naddy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35112891-naddy"><img alt="Naddy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464754630p2/35112891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1798851205?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Naddy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Naddy" href="/user/show/35112891-naddy">Naddy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1798851205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17515034587154826518">This is first which I have read with a backdrop of Punjab. Being a Punjabi, you pick up an Indian book and you got to know whole backdrop of the book is based in Punjab, you can imagine my feeling. Hullabaloo definitely hits a bit of nostalgia in me, starting from my childhood, getting up and seeing the maze of houses around your roof or adjoining houses with your own wall. There were so many incidents where I could relate to plots and events very well, Marriage especially food Ladooo, pranthas,</span>
  <span id="freeText17515034587154826518" style="display:none">This is first which I have read with a backdrop of Punjab. Being a Punjabi, you pick up an Indian book and you got to know whole backdrop of the book is based in Punjab, you can imagine my feeling. Hullabaloo definitely hits a bit of nostalgia in me, starting from my childhood, getting up and seeing the maze of houses around your roof or adjoining houses with your own wall. There were so many incidents where I could relate to plots and events very well, Marriage especially food Ladooo, pranthas, Babas, superstitions :) We all have a breaking points, mood swings when we want to run from mundane obligations, we feel like we are into one endless, everybody looks for some solace, some take refuge in Guava Orchard or someone like me in such books or mysteries……….. <br />Taking about Plot "Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard," is a tale of a young man named Sampath Chawla who lives in town Shahkot, Punjab He was born on the night a severe drought ends when a ferocious monsoon sweeps over the region. Immediately following his birth, the Swedish Red Cross makes a food drop right in front of Sampath's house. The people in the community of Shahkot are convinced that Sampath is destined to be an important man. Not only does he bring on the much needed rain, he causes the Red Cross, flying over Shahkot, to drop food for the hungry people. Sampath's eccentric mother and demanding father are not so sure about their baby becoming a great man. To Kulfi, his mother, he is an odd looking alien with a large brown birthmark on his face.<br /><i><br />“It was a terrible thing to be awake while some people flew, carrying the world over his head, and others slept, claiming it from under his feet.” </i><br /><br />Twenty years later, Sampath has not yet lived up to the prediction of greatness. In fact, he is anything but great. His father tries to counsel him on how he can get a better job or at least a raise in salary at the Post Office where he works. Sampath pays no attention to his father's advice. At the Post Office, Sampath spends hours alone, steaming open letters written to and from neighbors and by so doing, he learns a great deal about their personal lives and their secrets.<br />A great controversy develops about the monkeys who live in the guava tree with Sampath. The large monkeys attack people and often steal alcohol and get very drunk. The town officials are determined to rid the town of the monkeys but Sampath decides to save his lovely friends. How he saves them is both surprising and magical. When his father looks for him, he finds a large guava in his place with a brown mark on its skin. Sampath is able to save the monkeys and find a new home for himself; Sampath's dream to become part of the nature becomes a reality.<br /><br />I believe there is certain kindness and honesty in writing and it is mix with right amount of humor. There are few incidents which can literally give you hysteria of laughter. After reading so much classics and heavy books I wanted to read something light and this book serves the right purpose. The only things which I didn’t like about this book, there are few scenes were bit dramatized at times, I feel it is with every Indian author, they want the books to adapt into movies, which barely happens in India if it is not Chetan bhagat. The ending of the book was also quite predictable. <br /><br />So I will sign off with <b>3</b>/5. I think will give it try to The Inheritance of loss too. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17515034587154826518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sasha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2132616-sasha"><img alt="Sasha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1237873379p2/2132616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50376758?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          added it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50376758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3878600258640438283">Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard was lying in my office library and on a slow work day I decided to give it a read. Written by Kiran Desai, who even jointly won the Betty Trask award for it, Hullabaloo is everything that a book should never be.<br/><br/>It is pretentious, written in a direct vernacular to English translation style and has a story that I had to try very hard to stay connected to.<br/><br/>What is it about? About a Monkey Baba. A young boy from a village called Shahkot suffers from a madness gene pass</span>
  <span id="freeText3878600258640438283" style="display:none">Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard was lying in my office library and on a slow work day I decided to give it a read. Written by Kiran Desai, who even jointly won the Betty Trask award for it, Hullabaloo is everything that a book should never be.<br /><br />It is pretentious, written in a direct vernacular to English translation style and has a story that I had to try very hard to stay connected to.<br /><br />What is it about? About a Monkey Baba. A young boy from a village called Shahkot suffers from a madness gene passed down to him from his mother's side of the family. This boy, Sampat, can't cut it in the working world, so he runs away and decides to live in a Guava orchard. There he makes monkey friends. <br /><br />He utters nonsensical things so everyone thinks he must be from another world and declare the guava orchard a hermitage. Half way through the book, I had to try very hard to continue reading. But I had read so much about the Desai mother-daughter duo from our topline newspapers that I felt I should finish it.<br /><br />The only character I liked was Kulfi, Sampat's mother, who's obssessed with cooking up exotic dishes for her son. Her character reminded me of Tita from Like Water For Chocolate, but only just.<br /><br />I hope Inheritance of Loss, by the same author, is a better book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3878600258640438283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_161608808" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161608808" />
    <a title="Marianne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5067228-marianne"><img alt="Marianne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1303027210p2/5067228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161608808?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marianne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marianne" href="/user/show/5067228-marianne">Marianne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571195718" href="/book/show/916891.Hullabaloo_in_the_Guava_Orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161608808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7766697986360352494">Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard is the first novel by Kiran Desai. In the town of Shahkot, in the shadow of the Himalayan foothills, lives Sampath Chawla, a bored, dreamy Post Office clerk distinguishing himself with lacklustre career ambitions. When he manages to lose his job, his father, Mr Chawla, despairs that his son will ever amount to anything; his mother, Kulfi, says little, but then, she did come from a mad family; his sister Pinky finds him irritating and exasperating; his paternal gra</span>
  <span id="freeText7766697986360352494" style="display:none">Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard is the first novel by Kiran Desai. In the town of Shahkot, in the shadow of the Himalayan foothills, lives Sampath Chawla, a bored, dreamy Post Office clerk distinguishing himself with lacklustre career ambitions. When he manages to lose his job, his father, Mr Chawla, despairs that his son will ever amount to anything; his mother, Kulfi, says little, but then, she did come from a mad family; his sister Pinky finds him irritating and exasperating; his paternal grandmother, Ammaji, however, is convinced he will come good. Overwhelmed by the attention, Sampath decides to climb a tree in the Guava Orchard to be alone, to clear his thoughts, a deed that, unfortunately for Sampath, has quite the opposite effect. Convinced he is a hermit, people gather to hear his thoughts: this sets in motion events that will affect not only Sampath and his family, but the people of the district, the Chief Medical Officer, the Superintendent of Police, the Army Brigadier, the University researcher, the District Collector and even a spy from the Atheist Society. This novel has a cast of amusing characters, a plot with a few surprises and is filled with wonderful prose like: “A passing car sent its searchlight-glare crazy and liquid over the sides of the buildings and into the trees, revealing not the colours, the daylight solidity of things, but a world of dark gaps cut from an empty skin of light”. Desai is skilled at creating atmosphere and this novel has a uniquely Indian feel. This novel was a pleasure to read and it is easy to see why it won the Betty Trask Award in 1998.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7766697986360352494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16365952882648023810">Chapter 3 +4 :<br/><br/>The life of the Indian family is told. The father who gives commands, the children trying to keep up with his demands and the crazy mother thinking of food.<br/><br/>All in all chapter 3 reads itself fluently and it gives a nice, but a bit strange and boring view over the life of a paysan family in the Himalaya.<br/><br/>In the next chapter the life in the city Shahkot is told, little, but interesting and funny things happening at the end Sampath looses his work.<br/><br/><br/>The more I'm reading the more I come in</span>
  <span id="freeText16365952882648023810" style="display:none">Chapter 3 +4 :<br /><br />The life of the Indian family is told. The father who gives commands, the children trying to keep up with his demands and the crazy mother thinking of food.<br /><br />All in all chapter 3 reads itself fluently and it gives a nice, but a bit strange and boring view over the life of a paysan family in the Himalaya.<br /><br />In the next chapter the life in the city Shahkot is told, little, but interesting and funny things happening at the end Sampath looses his work.<br /><br /><br />The more I'm reading the more I come into the Indian mood and the more I like it. <br /><br />Slowly Kiran Desai is linking the at the beginning strangly appearing different scenes. For example the absurd seeming jump between the live in the family and then suddenly 20 years later a wedding in a post office. <br />I find it good that not everything is clear and that she is revealing and opening the story slowly.<br />It keeps the tension and makes me want to continue the book. <br /><br /><br />7.12<br />Now I'm really inside the book and with Kiran Desai's very fluent writing style reading the book is a real joy. Desai is writing with a lot of humor and the funny events and ideas she introduces, like the monkeys, are making me enjoy the book. <br /><br /><br />Another thing I like is how she describes the people, the reader learns a lot and the main character Sampath seems more and more inteligent. The book is sill funny and nice to read.<br /><br /></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer196377468" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13722873946349215508">It was dragging to start with, where it looked like the author was padding out the narrative unnecessarily while the plot itself stagnated, but 1/3 into the book, things got a lot more interesting.<br/><br/><br/><br/>This book takes a light hearted approach to one of India's many charms: gurus, the individuals who pursue/have attained enlightenment, and how they can end up being one without claiming anything to that effect. Young Sampath is not successful at school nor work, hates being stuck in a post office job h</span>
  <span id="freeText13722873946349215508" style="display:none">It was dragging to start with, where it looked like the author was padding out the narrative unnecessarily while the plot itself stagnated, but 1/3 into the book, things got a lot more interesting.<br /><br /><br /><br />This book takes a light hearted approach to one of India's many charms: gurus, the individuals who pursue/have attained enlightenment, and how they can end up being one without claiming anything to that effect. Young Sampath is not successful at school nor work, hates being stuck in a post office job his father secured for him and longs to just be left alone. In a desperation to escape all the expectations that is placed on him (which he had no intentions of fulfilling), one day he climbs a guava tree and stays there. Soon enough he attracks the attention of many, humans and beasts alike, but not all for a good end.<br /><br /><br /><br />Desai's quirky imagination, especially in regard to Sampath's dreamy, unusual mother, Kulfi does offer a rich imagery and a certain mystical quality to the book. Although her style of writing is yet to match the masters of post-collonial authors such as Salman Rushdie and Arundathi Roy, she's definitely one to watch out for.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13722873946349215508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12427315565582420489" style="display:none">since the book was set in shahkot, north india(the capital of divine religious cultures and morals) one would expect the book to be have a wide range of religious themes, settings etc however desai employs a much different setting. she completely turns around and lowers the prestige pattern of hinduism as a religion and culture that was set in books known for this such as 'a passage to india'.<br /> the book's main genre is comedy and that is the device she uses in potraying the indian beliefs. the author potrays them as corupt and totally commercialised people not the simple non-buereaucratical peopole. desai openely says this; " how many hermits were secretly wealthy? how many holy men were not at all the beggers they appeared to be? "<br />to turn around a corrupt and illegal act of reading through peoples mail as a spiritual proclaimation conveys the insatiable appetite for gurus <br /> when she turns around the religious theme. it is quite predictable since the title itself speaks about what one would expect before reading thus no criticism should be put on its religious setting</span>

Who fell in the cauldron though!?!</span>

<div id="review_161621492" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161621492" />
    <a title="Tze-Wen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60762-tze-wen"><img alt="Tze-Wen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468659682p2/60762.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161621492?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tze-Wen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tze-Wen" href="/user/show/60762-tze-wen">Tze-Wen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780571193363" href="/book/show/916895.Hullabaloo_In_The_Guava_Orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60762-tze-wen?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60762-tze-wen?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60762-tze-wen?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60762-tze-wen?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161621492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11467213042736533333"><i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i> was a fun, quick read, full of self-deprecating humor and a sprinkle of fairy(tale) dust. There is a very small undercurrent of seriousness (for example the few pages on how to choose a bride, and how prospective brides should behave), but otherwise it's one big drama that reminds one of, for want of any better comparison, a Bollywood movie. As long as you don't go looking for hidden meanings, you will be able to sit back and enjoy it as a lighthearted, frivolous </span>
  <span id="freeText11467213042736533333" style="display:none"><i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i> was a fun, quick read, full of self-deprecating humor and a sprinkle of fairy(tale) dust. There is a very small undercurrent of seriousness (for example the few pages on how to choose a bride, and how prospective brides should behave), but otherwise it's one big drama that reminds one of, for want of any better comparison, a Bollywood movie. As long as you don't go looking for hidden meanings, you will be able to sit back and enjoy it as a lighthearted, frivolous read. <br />Sampath believes he is truly better off up in a tree, but he only starts feeling comfortable and at ease when his family comes to support his new lifestyle; they lift up his food, make him a hammock, fix him a parasol. Even his bath water and toilet pots are lifted up and down the length of the guava tree. So, when he starts lamenting his loss of freedom, he clearly is romanticizing his hermit life. The same goes for his sister Pinky, who feels truly miserable living away from town. Therefore, on the few chances she gets to enter village life again, she dresses up like a Bollywood movie star, blinding everyone in her path with masses of fake jewelry. When she falls in love with the ice cream seller, the poor boy does not know how to react (which is only natural when the aforementioned girl just bit off your ear). In spite of this, he is soon enraptured and ready to run away with his star-crossed bride. However, when he sets eyes on the pretty, fair skinned bride his family has chosen for him, doubt enters his mind... And the entire monkey episode is truly out of this world: their booze hunting antics that result in the entire town being at odds with one another, it was hilarious to me. <br />Finally, I love Kulfi's obsession with food, which is reawakened after the family moves to the guava tree. Her brilliant, unorthodox cooking makes her a special lady in my eyes, and not a crazy one!<br /><br /><blockquote>"[...] Kulfi sliced and pounded, ground and smashed, cut and chopped in a chaos of ingredients and dishes. 'Cumin, quail, mustard seeds, pomelo rind,' she muttered as she cooked, 'Fennel, coriander, sour mango. Pandanus flour, lichen and perfumed kewra. Colocassia leaves, custard apple, winter melon, bitter gourd. Khas root, sandalwood, ash gourd, fenugreek greens. Snake-gourd, banana flowers, spider leaf, lotus root...'<br />She was producing meals so intricate, they were cooked sometimes with a hundred ingredients, balanced precariously within a complicated and delicate mesh of spices - marvelous triumphs of the complex and delicate art of seasoning. A single grain of one thing, a bud of another, a moist fingertip dipped lightly into a small vial and then into the bubbling pot; a thimble full, a matchbox full, a coconut shell full of dark crimson and deep violet, of dusty yellow spice, the entire concoction simmered sometimes for a day or two on coals that emitted only a glimmer of faint heat or that roared like a furnace as she fanned them with a palm leaf."<br /><br /><i>- Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i><br /></blockquote><br /><br />If I had to name one negative point, I'd have to say that the ending was rather sudden. Agreed, perhaps no better ending was possible, but it left me wondering what exactly fell into the pot?</span>
  <a data-text-id="11467213042736533333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_239517303" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/239517303" />
    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5095361-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320167322p2/5095361.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239517303?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/5095361-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239517303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer325507799297649808">Wow. I did not expect "Hullabaloo" to turn out like that. It was so surprising and a page turner by the end of the book, much unlike the beginning. In the middle, the book was a bit confusing with all the new chapters being introduced but you seem to understand everything by the end which was clever of Desai, how she brang everything together at the end. Sampath is an unusual character trying to find himself so he climbs a guava tree an decides to live there. His father is against this but his m</span>
  <span id="freeText325507799297649808" style="display:none">Wow. I did not expect "Hullabaloo" to turn out like that. It was so surprising and a page turner by the end of the book, much unlike the beginning. In the middle, the book was a bit confusing with all the new chapters being introduced but you seem to understand everything by the end which was clever of Desai, how she brang everything together at the end. Sampath is an unusual character trying to find himself so he climbs a guava tree an decides to live there. His father is against this but his mother and grandmother don't seem to mind. Everything seems to be going great and people worship him like a god but things start to go downhill when the monkeys get drunk. I never imagined Sampath to take such a downturn after the monkeys drunken rage. Also, what I found intriging was the ending. I still havn't quite figuref out what ended up in the pot. Was it the guava or was it Sampath? Or was the guava Sampath? I really liked Pinky eventhough shes a bit strange. Her breif fling with the Hungry Hop boy was funny and for me, was something I especially looked forward to seeing how it turned out. I really liked this book. Its so different from 1984 and gave us a break from all the seriousness. But I struggle to find the point. Is it just a fictional book that is nice to read or does it have a message? I actually think the relationship between Sampath and the monkeys is like that of a leader and his people. We can also relate it to the leaders of the arab worlds, whose people revolted against them much like the monkeys when they were drunk. However by the end of the book, the relationship between them is almost like that of a parent to their children since Sampath is very hurt and distraught at the thought of his monkeys being captured. This book was really nice to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="325507799297649808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_240701445" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/240701445" />
    <a title="Frédéric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5090395-fr-d-ric"><img alt="Frédéric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321963448p2/5090395.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/240701445?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frédéric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frédéric" href="/user/show/5090395-fr-d-ric">Frédéric</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240701445" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5210807913039136895">I thought the story till now was interesting, though I'm still confused about the significanse of everything. However, I'm sure that they'll be revealed soon enough and that the story will have a mind-capturing pace and beat. I deeply appreciate the relation to nature in the story; the monsoon and the heatwave before. The authors descriptive language is really one to admire because of the use of metaphors and similies, especially the fashion in which the author depicted the rain, as peacockishly</span>
  <span id="freeText5210807913039136895" style="display:none">I thought the story till now was interesting, though I'm still confused about the significanse of everything. However, I'm sure that they'll be revealed soon enough and that the story will have a mind-capturing pace and beat. I deeply appreciate the relation to nature in the story; the monsoon and the heatwave before. The authors descriptive language is really one to admire because of the use of metaphors and similies, especially the fashion in which the author depicted the rain, as peacockishly as ever, its importance in being the sole life giver. How the people all halted their affairs and ran towards the windows to verify their hopes. How the children looked in awe and moved with the vibrant jerks that anyone can infer as happiness. The author made it an interesting chapter to read. Though I didn't really enjoy reading about the perplexity and the confusion the people had about Kulfi's sentiments and actions. I think it had strechted too long to even think of considering reading it again and savoring it. No, it had strechted too long and it wasn't pleasing. Moreover, the reader even till the end of the second chapter isn't given any background information on Kulfi and her family. The reader will read and learn about her husband Mr. Chawla and her mother-in-law, Ammaji. I don't know, I can't foresee any event or impotance until I am given some ligitimate details on the authors characters. I enjoyed the story a lot, and I'm anticipating to read more.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5210807913039136895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText15981979961666485674" style="display:none">this book was rather entertaining... it helps the mind look beyond the obvious and try to make sense of little details that at first one would consider insignificant. it certainly changed my perception of the world or any other piece of literature for that matter... Miss Randa if you're reading this, then yes i have become under the influence of over analytical thinking thank you :P <br />the book asks of each person what he/she might consider the meaning or purpose of their life, and as odd and strange as it might seem i think the author meant to tell us that we can't pin point the meaning of life as a general or internationally known concept for it might differ for each person and that's why this story highlighted different parts of different character's lives. Any person can make sense of the world, it's the person who questions that sense who is really thinking of his purpose in life and is attempting to live it like any other person. Eventually we all ask ourselves weather to live fully we must put our personal desires ahead of societies expectations. Well there can never be a definite answer for that because society is made up of different individuals and its our ability to live and cope with each other despite our differences which actually makes us humane. So perhaps what the author really is trying to say is that everyone should live his life as humanely as possible with everything god has given him... a natural world, and the ability to build another world around him.</span>

    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6288717-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239573415?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/6288717-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239573415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8084112283235402120">The first two chapters of “Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard” by Kiran Desai caught my attention, and left me eager to know how the story will unfold. As I progressed I started to find the book completely meaningless, and felt like the novel was getting nowhere. While reading I thought all the mischief the monkeys were causing we quite humorous but stupid at the same time. I disliked the book so much to an extent that if reading it wasn’t obligatory I would of given up on the book ages ago, but I’</span>
  <span id="freeText8084112283235402120" style="display:none">The first two chapters of “Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard” by Kiran Desai caught my attention, and left me eager to know how the story will unfold. As I progressed I started to find the book completely meaningless, and felt like the novel was getting nowhere. While reading I thought all the mischief the monkeys were causing we quite humorous but stupid at the same time. I disliked the book so much to an extent that if reading it wasn’t obligatory I would of given up on the book ages ago, but I’m glad I didn’t! I would of never thought that the monkeys getting drunk would have been such a huge problem, but that moment resulted in a complete turnaround in the lives of all the citizens of Shahkot. My favorite character throughout the novel has to be Pinky, because most of the interesting bits happened when she was around, and her little romance with the Hungry Hop Boy was quite amusing. I also enjoyed the character of the Chief Medical Officer. The ending left me quite confused. Was Sampath in the tree? Does Sampath die?<br />Overall the novel had its ups and downs, the exciting ending doesn’t really make up for the really slow and meaningless majority of the novel, but it leaves readers with something to think about.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8084112283235402120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Sampath Chawla has grown up to become quite a disappointment to his family. He works in a post office where he spends most of his time reading the mail that comes through the office.   <span id="freeText6292246701276052458" style="display:none">Everything I want in a book: engaging characters, a bit of a plot, a little fun, a tiny bit of underlying seriousness. Sampath Chawla has grown up to become quite a disappointment to his family. He works in a post office where he spends most of his time reading the mail that comes through the office. His father is in despair; Sampath has no ambition. One day, Sampath leaves his job and, almost without thinking much about it, climbs up into a guava tree. He doesn't come down. As time passes, a mythology about Sampath begins to develop, and villagers come to sit under his tree, observe Sampath's tranquil state, and ask questions of Sampath. Sampath becomes known as a holy man. <br /><br />There's more, involving monkeys and romance, bitten-off ears and spies for an atheist group, but you get the idea. Fun, but not necessarily frivolous. </span>

  <span id="freeText7849504262959497722" style="display:none">Well I must say putting down this book was harder than picking it up the first time, towards the end I had grown fond of the book and had found it very diverting, a sharp contrast to when I was posting comments on how it was boring, I feel as if the author wrote this as “India for dummies “ .         <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer8319969371927421988">From the beginning of the book to the middle I found the book boring. However as I continued to read the book, it slowly but surely became interesting. To be honest if I was given this book by a person and they told me to read it, I would have put it down by the first chapter.I think that the monkeys getting drunk was nothing big but just an idea to add a bit of humor into the book.<br/>No one believes the grandmother when she tells them that Sampath will be successful but he is taking a bit more tim</span>
  <span id="freeText8319969371927421988" style="display:none">From the beginning of the book to the middle I found the book boring. However as I continued to read the book, it slowly but surely became interesting. To be honest if I was given this book by a person and they told me to read it, I would have put it down by the first chapter.I think that the monkeys getting drunk was nothing big but just an idea to add a bit of humor into the book.<br />No one believes the grandmother when she tells them that Sampath will be successful but he is taking a bit more time than everybody else.She was able to prove her point when Sampath climbs the Guava Orchard looking and hoping for peace and all of a sudden out of the blue he becomes famous as a Holy Man. <br />The ending was unexpected and I didn't think I would enjoy this book but I was proven wrong. I'm glad you made us read this book because now I know to have patience while reading a book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8319969371927421988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_271734'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/271734.Fasting_Feasting"><img alt="Fasting, Feasting" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1430821849l/271734._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BL8WtsVYSAlIIdHRRjYKCs1UITcZOngId6sF9/j0cjx3qww7/aop1aHPXRWyyDDpRG/0sYUgpfPsxpsbsZ36bA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3d6bBxK9PJvikXhfM4usoGrioqyVzvBjzdJRG/QiSfmuyoGKKk9dRwt/9JvHdZZD49l3KgnULZhWv8/3vUvBqQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Servant. Philosopher. Entrepreneur. Murderer. Over the course of seven nights, by the scattered light of a preposterous chandelier, Balram tells us the terrible and transfixing story of how he came to be a success in life—having nothing but his own wits to help him along. \n\nBorn in the dark heart of India, Balram gets a break when he is hired as a driver for his village\'s wealthiest man, two house Pomeranians (Puddles and Cuddles), and the rich man\'s (very unlucky) son. From behind the wheel of their Honda City car, Balram\'s new world is a revelation. While his peers flip through the pages of Murder Weekly (&quot;Love -- Rape -- Revenge!&quot;), barter for girls, drink liquor (Thunderbolt), and perpetuate the Great Rooster Coop of Indian society, Balram watches his employers bribe foreign ministers for tax breaks, barter for girls, drink liquor (single-malt whiskey), and play their own role in the Rooster Coop. Balram learns how to siphon gas, deal with corrupt mechanics, and refill and resell Johnnie Walker Black Label bottles (all but one). He also finds a way out of the Coop that no one else inside it can perceive. \n\nBalram\'s eyes penetrate India as few outsiders can: the cockroaches and the call centers; the prostitutes and the worshippers; the ancient and Internet cultures; the water buffalo and, trapped in so many kinds of cages that escape is (almost) impossible, the white tiger. And with a charisma as undeniable as it is unexpected, Balram teaches us that religion doesn\'t create virtue, and money doesn\'t solve every problem -- but decency can still be found in a corrupt world, and you can get what you want out of life if you eavesdrop on the right conversations. \n\nThe White Tiger recalls The Death of Vishnu and Bangkok 8 in ambition, scope, and narrative genius, with a mischief and personality all its own. Amoral, irreverent, deeply endearing, and utterly contemporary, this novel is an international publishing sensation —and a startling, provocative debut.<\/span>\n  <a He was brought up in India in the apparently vigorous but dying Raj and was sent by his family in the 1930s to Berlin -- though he could not speak a word of German -- to study medicine and dentistry. It was here, before he migrated to Britain, that Shanti\'s path first crossed that of his future wife. \n\nHelga Gerda Caro, known to everyone as &quot;Henny&quot; was also born in 1908, in Berlin, to a Jewish family -- cultured, patriotic, and intensely German. When the family decided to take Shanti as a lodger, Henny\'s first reaction was, &quot;Don\'t take the black man!&quot; But a friendship flowered, and when Henny fled Hitler\'s Germany for England just one month before war broke out, she was met at Victoria Station by the only person in the country she knew: Shanti. \n\nVikram Seth has woven together their astonishing story, which recounts the arrival into this childless couple\'s lives of their great-nephew from India -- the teenage student Vikram Seth. The result is an extraordinary tapestry of India, the Third Reich and the Second World War, Auschwitz and the Holocaust, Israel and Palestine, postwar Germany and 1970s Britain. \n\nTwo Lives is both a history of a violent century seen through the eyes of two survivors and an intimate portrait of their friendship, marriage, and abiding yet complex love. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10114903788566013591">Kiran Desai is an Indian author who is a citizen of India and a permanent resident of the United States. She is the daughter of the noted author <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8841.Anita_Desai' title='Anita Desai' rel='nofollow noopener'>Anita Desai</a>.<br/><br/>Desai's first novel, <i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i> (1998), gained accolades from notable figures including Salman Rushdie, and went on to receive the Betty Trask Award. Her second novel, <i>The Inheritance of Loss</i> (2006), won the 2006 Man Booke</span>
  <span id="freeText10114903788566013591" style="display:none">Kiran Desai is an Indian author who is a citizen of India and a permanent resident of the United States. She is the daughter of the noted author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8841.Anita_Desai" title="Anita Desai" rel="nofollow noopener">Anita Desai</a>.<br /><br />Desai's first novel, <i>Hullabaloo in the Guava Orchard</i> (1998), gained accolades from notable figures including Salman Rushdie, and went on to receive the Betty Trask Award. Her second novel, <i>The Inheritance of Loss</i> (2006), won the 2006 Man Booker Prize and the National Book Critics Circle Fiction Award. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10114903788566013591" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
